Soc. 160, A3131âA3137 (2013)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1794_3">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vassilaras, P. et al. CommunicationâO3-type layered oxide with a quaternary transition metal composition for Na-ion battery cathodes: NaTi0.25Fe0.25Co0.25Ni0.25O2. J. Electrochem. Soc. 164, A3484âA3486 (2017)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1794_4">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hwang, J.-Y., Yu, T.-Y. &amp; Sun, Y.-K. Simultaneous MgO coating and Mg doping of Na[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 cathode: facile and customizable approach to high-voltage sodium-ion batteries. J. Mater. Chem. A 6, 16854â16862 (2018)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1794_5">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Song, J. et al. Controlling surface phase transition and chemical reactivity of O3-layered metal oxide cathodes for high-performance Na-ion batteries. ACS Energy Lett. 5, 1718â1725 (2020)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1797">51</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The present O3-type material stands apart from established O3-type cathodes, showing the diverse compositional and structural chemistry inherent in layered Na-ion oxides that plays crucial roles in impacting the electrochemical properties. The significance of the P3-type dominated solidâsolution transformation lies in its ability to suppress detrimental TM migration, foster high Na-ion diffusivity, minimize volume changes and offer notable advantages over the P2-type structure. This is attributed to its distinct oxygen stacking sequence (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#MOESM1">21</a>), leading to enhanced reversibility compared with previously reported cathodes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Talaie, E., Duffort, V., Smith, H. L., Fultz, B. &amp; Nazar, L. F. Structure of the high voltage phase of layered P2-Na2/3-z[Mn1/2Fe1/2]O2 and the positive effect of Ni substitution on its stability. Energy Environ. Sci. 8, 2512â2523 (2015)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1818">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Mortemard de Boisse, B., Carlier, D., Guignard, M., Bourgeois, L. &amp; Delmas, C. P2-NaxMn1/2Fe1/2O2 phase used as positive electrode in Na batteries: structural changes induced by the electrochemical (de)intercalation process. Inorg. Chem. 53, 11197â11205 (2014)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1821">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Yabuuchi, N. et al. P2-type Nax[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 made from earth-abundant elements for rechargeable Na batteries. Nat. Mater. 11, 512â517 (2012)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1824">42</a></sup>. To gain more understanding, we evaluated the role of TMs and Na compositions. The first observation is that the present O3-type Na<sub>5/6</sub>Li<sub>2/27</sub>Ni<sub>8/27</sub>Mn<sub>11/27</sub>Ti<sub>6/27</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material only differs slightly in TM composition while having the same Na composition compared with the P2-type Na<sub>5/6</sub>Li<sub>2/27</sub>Ni<sub>8/27</sub>Mn<sub>17/27</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, whereas the differences in the phase transformation mechanism and electrochemical performance are enormous. The partial substitution of Mn<sup>4+</sup> by Ti<sup>4+</sup> impacts neither the redox capability nor the Na capacity (although lowering weight); however, it induces a transformation from the P2- to O3-type phases. Introducing Ti<sup>4+</sup> lowers the weighted ionic potential at the TMs position, which decreases the TMâO interaction, thus increasing the <i>d</i><sub>(OâTMâO)</sub> distance and decreasing the ratio of the <i>d</i><sub>(OâNaâO)</sub> and <i>d</i><sub>(OâTMâO)</sub> distances. This flips the balance towards a preference for the O3-type structure, located near the boundary with the P2-type structure in the cationic phase map (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#Fig1">1c</a>). The consequence is an O3-type material with a relatively large <i>d</i><sub>(OâNaâO)</sub> interlayer space, which is a typical characteristic of P-type stacking structures. It is intriguing to consider the role of this in inducing the P3-type phase transformation upon charging. In the composition, Li<sup>+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> have relatively small charge numbers but a larger ionic potential<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Zhao, C. et al. Rational design of layered oxide materials for sodium-ion batteries. Science 370, 708â711 (2020)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1883">7</a></sup>, while Mn<sup>4+</sup> has a relatively large charge number and also a larger ionic potential compared with the other 3<i>d</i> ions. This combination, while considering charge neutrality, is responsible for a relatively large weighted average ionic potential for an O3-type material at the TM position<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Zhao, C. et al. Rational design of layered oxide materials for sodium-ion batteries. Science 370, 708â711 (2020)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d908765e1893">7</a></sup>, which maximizes the <i>d</i><sub>(OâNaâO)</sub> interlayer distance typical for P-type structures. Upon a small decrease in Na-ion composition during charging, accompanied by an increase in the Ni oxidation state, the decreased Na-ion shielding and the increased TMâO interaction will result in an increased <i>d</i><sub>(OâNaâO)</sub> distance and decreased <i>d</i><sub>(OâTMâO)</sub> distance, which is suggested to promote the P3-dominated phase transition. This indicates that both the detailed TM composition as well as Na composition play a decisive role in the phase transition behaviour upon charging.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Furthermore, the cationic phase map generalizes these structural characteristics through the interslab interactions, providing guidance on the relationship between composition and phase transition categories (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#Fig5">5</a>). A larger TM weighted average ionic potential (<span class="mathjax-tex">\(\overline{{{\varPhi}}_{\rm{TM}}}\)</span>) implies a higher charge density at the surface of TM layers; this increases the interlayer electrostatic repulsion, resulting in more covalent TMâO bonds and an increased <i>d</i><sub>(OâNaâO)</sub> distance, which we suggest to promote the P3-dominated phase transition upon charging (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#Fig5">5</a>). Here, the Na composition plays a crucial role as a smaller Na content (&lt;5/6) reduces the screening between TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, which favours the P2-type structure, albeit compromising the available capacity. Conversely, increasing Na composition (&gt;5/6) favours O3-type structures. To promote the P-type large <i>d</i><sub>(OâNaâO)</sub> distance in these structures, aiming to induce P3-type dominated transition requires increasing the <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\overline{{{{\varPhi}}}_{\rm{TM}}}\)</span> (typically increasing <i>d</i><sub>(OâNaâO)</sub>). However, this is difficult to achieve without introducing a higher TM oxidation state, which intrinsically opposes a large Na content. Through these guidelines, we argue that P3-dominated phase transitions in O3-type materials will occur for intermediate Na compositions, located near the boundary to carry P-type structural motifs, as indicated by the red region in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#Fig5">5</a>. This represents a relatively focused region in phase space, centred around the presented material. Reducing the <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\overline{{{{\varPhi}}}_{\rm{TM}}}\)</span> or/and increasing Na composition promotes the formation of O3-type structures, first presenting O3-type structures that are categorized as multistep phase transitions (green region) and finally, O3-type structures that are categorized as O3-type dominated phase transitions (blue region). These observations agree with the reported phase transitions marked in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#Fig5">5</a>, showing its generality. To provide more support that a compositional change can induce the P3-dominated phase transition, an additional O3-type composition was prepared as shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#Fig5">5</a>. Consistently, this composition displays the P3-type dominated phase transition (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#MOESM1">22</a>), supporting the proposed design principles.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Cationic potential phase map indicating the phase transformation mechanisms of O3-type Na-ion layered cathodes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Cationic potential phase map indicating the phase transformation mechanisms of O3-type Na-ion layered cathodes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41893-024-01266-1/MediaObjects/41893_2024_1266_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig5" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41893-024-01266-1/MediaObjects/41893_2024_1266_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure 5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="425"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The <i>x</i> axis represents the evolution of the average TM ionic potential <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\overline{{\varPhi}_{\rm{TM}}}\)</span>, which at a constant Na content (equivalent to a constant average Na ionic potential <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\overline{{\varPhi}_{\rm{Na}}}\)</span>) corresponds to a change in <i>d</i><sub>(OâNaâO)</sub>, promoting different phase transition behaviour. The <i>y</i> axis represents the average Na ionic potential which impacts the phase transition behaviour through the interlayer screening. On the basis of the Na-ion layered composition, the cationic potential and average Na ionic potential can be calculated (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#MOESM1">11</a>). The coloured regions show the suggested regions for the three types of phase transition.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 5" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It is worth noting that a minor alteration in composition may not consistently lead to an effortless O3âP3 phase transition during charging. We argue that specific prerequisites within the initial P2-type composition must be met. The presence of a certain amount of Na is essential to facilitate the formation of an O3-type structure. According to existing literature, a P2-type Na-ion oxide typically contains a Na content ~&lt;5/6âmol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zhao, C. et al. Revealing high Na-content P2-type layered oxides as advanced sodium-ion cathodes. J. Am. Chem. Soc. 142, 5742â5750 (2020)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2180">13</a></sup>, while an O3-type oxide requires a minimum Na content of ~3/4âmol or higher<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wang, P.-F. et al. An abnormal 3.7 volt O3-type sodium-ion battery cathode. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. 57, 8178â8183 (2018)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2184">41</a></sup>. Thus, the Na composition that can lead to a facile O3âP3 phase transition can be inferred to fall between ~3/4 and 5/6âmol. Na compositions outside this range will not only affect the TM composition, but will also move the composition away from the phase boundary. Meanwhile, since the properties of TMs within materials strongly impact both cathode performance and structural characteristics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kubota, K. et al. Impact of Mg and Ti doping in O3 type NaNi1/2Mn1/2O2 on reversibility and phase transition during electrochemical Na intercalation. J. Mater. Chem. A 9, 12830â12844 (2021)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2188">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Han, M. H., Gonzalo, E., Casas-Cabanas, M. &amp; Rojo, T. Structural evolution and electrochemistry of monoclinic NaNiO2 upon the first cycling process. J. Power Sources 258, 266â271 (2014)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2191">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Delmas, C., Braconnier, J.-J., Fouassier, C. &amp; Hagenmuller, P. Electrochemical intercalation of sodium in NaxCoO2 bronzes. Solid State Ion. 3â4, 165â169 (1981)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2194">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou, Y.-N. et al. Phase transition behavior of NaCrO2 during sodium extraction studied by synchrotron-based X-ray diffraction and absorption spectroscopy. J. Mater. Chem. A 1, 11130â11134 (2013)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2197">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lee, E. et al. New Insights into the performance degradation of Fe-based layered oxides in sodium-ion batteries: instability of Fe3+/Fe4+ redox in Î±-NaFeO2. Chem. Mater. 27, 6755â6764 (2015)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2197_1">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Susanto, D. et al. Anionic redox activity as a key factor in the performance degradation of NaFeO2 cathodes for sodium ion batteries. Chem. Mater. 31, 3644â3651 (2019)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2200">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Zhao, C., Lu, Y., Chen, L. &amp; Hu, Y.-S. Ni-based cathode materials for Na-ion batteries. Nano Res. 12, 2018â2030 (2019)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2203">43</a></sup>, a judicious selection of TM composition is very important to ensure the development of high-quality cathode materials. In addition, concerning a low Na content upon charging, the interslab interaction of cathodes persists and can be assessed through the cationic potential method. However, when the cathode with a very low Na content reaches a higher state of charge, it may undergo oxygen redox reactions. During this process, the reduced negative charge of oxygen ions can lead to a decrease in the average anionic potential <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\overline{{{{\varPhi}}}_{\rm{anion}}}\)</span>. Moreover, this situation becomes increasingly intricate due to various factors that can contribute to structural changes, such as local structure distortions, stress and other influences. Consequently, depending solely on the cationic potential approach may not furnish a comprehensive guideline on the structural transition, given its reliance on straightforward calculations of the composition.</p><p>In summary, we make use of the diverse compositional space of layered Na-ion oxides to tune structural chemistry, resulting in new cathodes for SIBs that demonstrate fast charging in combination with a high average operation voltage and capacity retention. The results indicate that the P3-type-dominated solidâsolution reaction with open prismatic Na-ion diffusion pathways between the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs is responsible for the fast Na-ion transport and excellent reversibility. The proposed design strategy is expected to promote the development of economic and green electrode materials, facilitating the realization of highly sustainable high-energy-density batteries.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Materials</h3><p>The material, Na<sub>5/6</sub>Li<sub>2/27</sub>Ni<sub>8/27</sub>Mn<sub>11/27</sub>Ti<sub>6/27</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, was prepared by a solid-state reaction. Stoichiometric precursors of NaNO<sub>3</sub> (&gt;98%), NiO (&gt;98%), MnO<sub>2</sub> (99%), TiO<sub>2</sub> (99.5%) and Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (99.5%) were thoroughly mixed in an agate mortar in an Ar-filled glove box (H<sub>2</sub>Oâ&lt;â0.1âppm, O<sub>2</sub>â&lt;â0.1âppm) and pressed into pellets under a pressure of 10âMPa. An excess of 5âmol% NaNO<sub>3</sub> was added to compensate for evaporation at high temperatures. Then the precursor was calcined twice at 1,030âÂ°C in a poor O<sub>2</sub> atmosphere in a tubular furnace for 15âh with intermediate grinding and cooled to 150âÂ°C. The speeds for heating and cooling were 5âÂ°Câmin<sup>â1</sup> and 2âÂ°Câmin<sup>â1</sup>, respectively. After the heat treatment, the material was directly put into an Ar-filled glove box to prevent any moisture exposition. The as-synthesized material can be washed with absolute ethanol, followed by drying overnight at 100âÂ°C under vacuum in an Ar-filled glove box.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Materials characterization</h3><p>Morphologies of materials were measured on a cold field scanning electron microscope (SEM, HITACH-SU8010) equipped with energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS, IXRF SYSTEM, 550i). All presented images are representative of parallel measurements. XRD was performed using a Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer equipped with a Cu KÎ± radiation source (<i>Î»</i><sub>1</sub>â=â1.54060âÃ, <i>Î»</i><sub>2</sub>â=â1.54439âÃ at 40âkV and 40âmA) and a LynxEye_XE detector. Rietveld refinement of the XRD was carried out using the General Structure Analysis System software with the EXPGUI software interface. A specially designed Swagelok cell equipped with an X-ray transparent Al window was used for the in situ measurements on an XâPert Pro X-ray diffractometer (PANalytical) (<i>Î»</i><sub>1</sub>â=â1.54060âÃ, <i>Î»</i><sub>2</sub>â=â1.54439âÃ at 60âkV and 40âmA). CV measurements were conducted on an Autolab (PGSTAT302N) at different scan rates.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Neutron powder diffraction (NPD)</h3><p>NPD data were collected on a high-resolution power diffractometer at the China Advanced Research Reactor at the China Institute of Atomic Energy. The wavelength was 1.889âÃ with a scanning step of 0.075Â° at room temperature. The sample was filled into thin-wall vanadium containers (300âÂµm wall thickness and 10âmm diameter) in an Ar-filled glove box with indium metal wire. Diffraction data were obtained in the 2<i>Î¸</i> range from 5 to 145Â°.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Synchrotron X-ray diffraction (SXRD)</h3><p>SXRD data were collected at beamline 11-ID-C, Advanced Photon Source, Argonne National Laboratory, using an X-ray wavelength of 0.1173âÃ.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) characterization</h3><p>TEM experiments were performed on a STEM (JEM-ARM300F, JEOL) operated at 300âkV with a cold field emission gun and double Cs correctors. The microscope was equipped with Gatan OneView and K2 cameras for image recording. STEM images were taken with an electron dose rate of 50â500âe<sup>â</sup>âÃ<sup>â2</sup>âs<sup>â1</sup> with an exposure time of several seconds for each image, with a built-in drift correction function in GMS3 using the OneView and K2 camera. Analysis of the spectra was performed in Digital Micrograph.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">XAS</h3><p>Hard XAS experiments were acquired at the KMC-2 beamline of the synchrotron BESSY at Helmholtz-Zentrum Berlin, Germany, in transmission mode with a graded Si-Ge (111) double-crystal monochromator. About 65% of the maximum possible intensity of the beam was transmitted through the sample during the measurements to eliminate high-order harmonics. Energy calibration was performed by simultaneously measuring the spectra of a reference metal foil. The loading mass of the active materials was ~15âmgâcm<sup>â2</sup>. The raw data were processed and analysed using the Athena software package. The EXAFS signal was normalized by Fourier transformation in R space without phase shift correction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">RIXS</h3><p>RIXS experiments were recorded at the U41-PEAXIS beamline at Helmholtz-Zentrum Berlin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Schulz, C. et al. Characterization of the soft X-ray spectrometer PEAXIS at BESSY II. J. Synchrotron Radiat. 27, 238â249 (2020)." href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d908765e2375">44</a></sup>. All the samples were sealed in a specially designed container. During the test, samples were transferred into the vacuum chamber and not exposed to any air. Mapping data were collected using an ultra-high-efficiency spectrometer with an excitation energy step of 0.5âeV. Each spectrum was collected for 30âmin. The resolution of the emission energy was 0.08âeV.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Electrochemical measurements</h3><p>The cathodes were prepared by mixing 85 wt% active material with 5 wt% acetylene black (Denka black, Denki Kagaku Kogyo, average size 20~40ânm), 5 wt% carbon nanotube (XFNANO Materials, diameter 1~2ânm, length 0.5â10âÎ¼m) and 5 wt% polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) in an Ar-filled glove box for ~1âh. After that, the resultant was rolled into a self-standing film electrode with the loading mass of the active material being ~8âmgâcm<sup>â2</sup>. The prepared electrodes were dried overnight at 100âÂ°C under vacuum in an Ar-filled glove box and were then fabricated into CR2032 coin-type cells with pure sodium foil as the counter electrode in an Ar-filled glove box. Hard carbon anodes (Kuraray) were prepared by mixing hard carbon, sodium alginate binder and acetylene black at a weight ratio of 90:5:5, and the resulting slurry was cast and then dried overnight at 80âÂ°C under vacuum. Na salt (1.2âM), including 0.4âM NaPF<sub>6</sub> and 0.8âM NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate/ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate (PC/EC/DMCâ=â1:1:1 in volume) with fluoroethylene carbonate (10% volume) was used as the electrolyte. A glass fibre filter (Whatman GF/D) was used as the separator. The charge and discharge measurements were carried out using multichannel battery testing systems (Land CT2001A or Lanhe G340A) at room temperature. The GITT was measured by applying the repeated current pulses for 0.5âh at a current density of 0.1âC, followed by relaxation for 6âh.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41893-024-01266-1#MOESM2">Nature Portfolio Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>

id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>M.W. and Q.W. conceived the project. Q.W., D.Z., Z.Y. and C.Z. carried out the synthesis, material characterization and electrochemical measurements. H.G. collected the NPD data; L.W. and J.L. collected the SXRD data; and Q.W. and C.Z. interpreted the data. J.W. and X.B. performed the TEM measurement. D.Z., D.W. and G.S. collected and interpreted the XAS and RIXS data. All authors participated in discussing the results. Q.W., M.W., C.Z., J.L. and D.Z. prepared and revised the paper with inputs from all other authors.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
